<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="e8ae1b07c0bb4890a869f744c4542625"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="e8ae1b07c0bb4890a869f744c4542625">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'e9936cf3-08bd-410a-ab39-e3fe2fced9a4'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>State Key Laboratory for Modification of Chemical Fibers and Polymer Materials, College of Materials Science and Engineering, Donghua University, Shanghai, 201620 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Li/Xiao">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Wang/Fei" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a4" data-db-target-for="a4" aria-controls="a4" aria-haspopup="true" id="a4_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Fei Wang</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a4" aria-labelledby="a4_Ctrl" role="region" id="a4">
                     <p class="author-name">Fei Wang</p>
                     <p>Department of Materials Science, Fudan University, Shanghai, 200433 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Wang/Fei">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Xu/Guiyin" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a5" data-db-target-for="a5" aria-controls="a5" aria-haspopup="true" id="a5_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Guiyin Xu<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a5" aria-labelledby="a5_Ctrl" role="region" id="a5">
                     <p class="author-type mb-1">Corresponding Author</p>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d104348726" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>The oxidized cellulose separator provides strong dipole moments, facilitating the cleavage of P−F bonds to construct a solid electrolyte interphase rich in NaF. In addition, the separator prevents the reduction of organic solvents by attracting electrons, thereby inhibiting the formation of organic oligomers. Thus, it results in a smooth and robust interphase, which significantly reduces the interface impedance of the Na anode.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/819bae1b-16d0-4a30-8d83-853def9d23dd/anie202415283-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/819bae1b-16d0-4a30-8d83-853def9d23dd/anie202415283-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ecef0370-a9e3-49de-88ea-2d9e56a5bc28/anie202415283-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/819bae1b-16d0-4a30-8d83-853def9d23dd/anie202415283-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d104348729" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Uncontrollable side reactions at the metal interface have been identified as the root cause of the formation of a fragile solid electrolyte interphase, leading to irreversible sodium loss in sodium metal batteries. Here, we proposed an interface engineering strategy that employed a carboxyl functionalized cellulose separator to provide strong dipole moments and induce the cleavage of P−F bond to construct a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) rich in NaF. In addition, we employed nuclear magnetic resonance technology confirmed that the separator with strong dipole moments prevented the reduction of organic solvents by attracting electrons, thereby inhibiting the formation of organic oligomers. SEI with high NaF content and few oligomers is smooth and robust, obviously decreasing the interface impedance of the Na anode. The symmetric Na||Na cells, equipped with the functionalized separator, efficiently operated for 1400 hours with a stable 72 mV overpotential at 0.25 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, exhibiting low energy barrier and fast ion transport kinetics. The Na||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell also showed stable cycling performance, with the capacity remaining at 94.83 % of the initial capacity after 1000 cycles at 1C. The proposed separator could control the formation and composition of SEI, paving the way for the development of long-life sodium metal batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202415283-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202415283-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The large-scale energy storage of lithium-ion batteries is limited by the uneven distribution of lithium resources.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0001_R_d104348718e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> By contrast, sodium boasts abundant reserves that are 2–3 orders of magnitude greater than that of lithium, driving the development of sodium batteries. Sodium metal batteries (SMBs), with a high theoretical specific capacity of 1165 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and low redox potential of −2.714 V vs standard hydrogen electrode, have garnered significant attention in academia as a promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0004_R_d104348718e406" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> The plating/stripping process that occurs during the charge/discharge process is the main mechanism of sodium (Na) transport in SMBs. However, the concentration polarization and the uneven distribution of local current density causes huge volume expansion and dendrite growth in the sodium anode, which finally deteriorate the stability of interface and cause short circuit. In addition, the sodium anode reacts with the organic electrolyte, leading to the formation of a loose solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer riches in organic components, which has a large interfacial impedance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0007_R_d104348718e409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> The unstable SEI is susceptible to decomposition, exposing the fresh sodium metal that subsequently consumes additional electrolyte until it is entirely depleted.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0010_R_d104348718e412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Eng. J.">4</a></sup></span> The above issues have substantial impacts on the safety, performance, and longevity of sodium metal batteries, representing critical challenges that should be addressed before sodium battery technology can progress towards the practical implementation.</p>
         
         <p>Separator as one of the key components of the battery, provides a channel for ion transport, profoundly affecting the plating/stripping behavior at the interface of the sodium metal anode.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0011_R_d104348718e418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Storage Mater.">5</a></sup></span> However, traditional commercial nonpolar polyolefin separators have poor compatibility with the polar carbonate electrolytes, which result in the slow kinetics, thus leading to the low ionic transport and inhomogeneous sodium deposition.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0012_R_d104348718e421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">6</a></sup></span> Polar materials including polyimide, polyetherimide, and polyethersulfone have been developed for the preparation of separators to ensure compatibility with electrolytes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0002_R_d104348718e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1a - Adv. Mater.">1a</a>, <a href="#anie202415283-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0013_R_d104348718e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7">7</a></sup></span> Among numerous raw polar materials of separator, cellulose distinguishes itself as an ideal choice due to its low cost, excellent mechanical property, abundance of polar functional groups, and superior thermal stability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0017_R_d104348718e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> Li et al.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0021_R_d104348718e430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Energy Chem.">9</a></sup></span> successfully suppressed the lithium dendrite by utilizing a cellulose nanofiber separator with a tunable pore structure, thereby enhancing the cycling stability of batteries. Seo et al.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0022_R_d104348718e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Storage Mater.">10</a></sup></span> prepared chiral nematic liquid crystalline cellulose nanocrystal and coated them onto polyethylene (PE) substrate films, achieving uniform ion flux toward anode. In addition, the abundance of active hydroxyl groups in cellulose offers ample opportunities for further chemical modifications, enabling batteries with modified cellulose-based separators stable electrochemical behavior.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0023_R_d104348718e437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Polymer">11</a></sup></span> Polar groups such as sulfonate and amino have been reported to promote uniform ion flux, thereby inducing an uniform metal deposition.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0024_R_d104348718e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano-Micro Lett.">12</a></sup></span> However, the significant interface impedance caused by the formed loose SEI rich in organic components still exists, greatly affecting the kineties of charge transport.</p>
         
         <p>In this study, carboxyl groups, characterized by extremely strong electronegativity, are introduced into the plant cellulose-based separator skeleton, obtaining an increased dipole moment. Analysis of the SEI composition in the cycled symmetric Na||Na cells, along with quantum chemical simulations, demonstrates that the enhanced dipole moment of separator can facilitate the breakage of P−F bonds in NaPF<sub>6</sub>, resulting in SEI rich in NaF. In addition, the separator with strong dipole moments prevents the reduction of organic solvents by attracting electrons, preventing the formation of organic oligomers. The SEI with high NaF content and few oligomers is smooth and robust, obviously decreasing the interface impedance of the anode surface. Consequently, the cycling stability and Coulombic efficiency of cells utilizing the prepared high dipole moment cellulose separator are significantly improved. The symmetrical Na||Na cell remains a stable cycle over 1400 h and the Na||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cell delivers over 1000 stable cycles with an impressive 94.83 % capacity retention at a rate of 1C, surpassing the performance of cells employing commercial GF separators. Notably, the prepared separator can be efficiently produced on a large scale using the traditional wet process, offering a cost-effective and commercially valuable solution.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202415283-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202415283-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202415283-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202415283-sec-0003-title"> Design and Preparation of High Dipole Moment Separators</h3>
            
            <p>Cellulose, abundant in high reactive hydroxyl groups, offers the potential for the introduction of various functional groups. Density functional theory (DFT) calculation was employed to guide the selection of the modification route for cellulose. The oxidation, amination, and sulfonation reactions deemed appropriate for large-scale modification were selected to simulate their chemical properties.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0025_R_d104348718e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nano-Micro Lett.">13</a></sup></span> The electrostatic potential (ESP) and dipole moment of the raw and modified cellulose structure unit are shown in Figure 1a and b. The average ESP value of the oxidized cellulose structural unit is −0.54 kcal mol<sup>−1</sup>, which is much lower than that of raw (−0.35 kcal mol<sup>−1</sup>), aminated (1.22 kcal mol<sup>−1</sup>) and sulfonated (−0.39 kcal mol<sup>−1</sup>) cellulose structure units. Furthermore, the oxidized cellulose structural unit displays a highest dipole moment (4.97), indicating that the oxidation modification makes cellulose have a high atomic electronegativity difference. The binding energies of cellulose and −COOH with Na<sup>+</sup> (−1.83 eV) showed that −COOH has a higher binding energy and stronger interaction with Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0001">1c</a>). Molecular dynamic (MD) simulations were performed to estimate the effect of the oxidized cellulose compared to the raw cellulose on the solvation behavior of Na<sup>+</sup>. Six fixed chains of the raw cellulose and oxidized cellulose chains were added into the simulation boxes, as illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0001">1d</a> and <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0001">e</a>, and conducted statistical analysis. The corresponding radial distribution function (RDF) and coordination number of Na<sup>+</sup> and ethylene carbonate (EC)/diethyl carbonate (DEC) decreased obviously (Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0001">1f</a> and <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0001">g</a>), which could be attributed to the robust van der Waals forces and electrostatic interactions formed between Na<sup>+</sup> and oxidized cellulose chains, prompting the desolvation of Na<sup>+</sup> from the solvent molecules and its preference to move along the cellulose molecule chains.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0026_R_d104348718e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Sci.">14</a></sup></span> The desolvation energy of Na<sup>+</sup> in the presence of cellulose chains was accordingly simulated, where the primary solvation sheath consisted of three EC molecules and one DEC molecule. In the presence of the oxidized cellulose chain, the desolvation energy of Na<sup>+</sup> is 4.19 eV, which is significantly lower than that of the presence of cellulose chain (5.85 eV) (Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0001">1h</a>). This further confirms that the presence of carboxyl functionalized cellulose chains can weaken the binding between Na<sup>+</sup> and EC/DEC, facilitating the desolvation of Na<sup>+</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0027_R_d104348718e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Nano">15</a></sup></span> The above simulation validates that oxidized cellulose possesses abundant electron defects and a high dipole moment, profoundly influence the transport kineties and solvation structure of Na<sup>+</sup> in sodium ion batteries (SIBs). Therefore, the oxidation process was subsequently employed to modify the cellulose.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202415283-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c2fc862d-df8b-496b-a434-27e99af38cbf/anie202415283-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c2fc862d-df8b-496b-a434-27e99af38cbf/anie202415283-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/057b6e1a-2cf1-4c36-a964-9f791540e7cb/anie202415283-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c2fc862d-df8b-496b-a434-27e99af38cbf/anie202415283-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202415283-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202415283" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Design of the separator with high dipole moment. Distribution of ESP (a) and comparison of average ESP value and dipole moment (b) of raw cellulose, oxidized cellulose, aminated cellulose and sulfonated cellulose by the DFT calculation; (c) Binding energy of the raw cellulose chain between Na<sup>+</sup>, and oxidized cellulose chain between Na<sup>+</sup>; (d, e) Snapshots during MD simulation of the 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-EC/DEC with the existence of six fixed RC chains (d) and OC chains (e); (f, g) The RDFs and coordination number as deduced from the MD simulation; (h) Desolvation energy of [Na(EC)<sub>3</sub>(DEC)]<sup>+</sup> with the existence of a RC chain and an OC chain; (i) Digital image of an OC separator with dimensions of 15*31 cm<sup>2</sup>.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The environmentally friendly and efficient 2,2,6,6-Tetramethylpiperidinyloxy (TEMPO) method was selected for the preparation of oxidized cellulose. The TEMPO-mediated oxidation was employed to selectively oxidize −OH on C<sub>6</sub> to −COOH while preserving the cellulose structure.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0028_R_d104348718e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a></sup></span> Utilizing oxidized cellulose as the raw material and aramid nanofibers (ANFs) as the filler, a oxidized cellulose (OC) separator was prepared through the wet process. Additionally, using the same preparation method and with unmodified raw cellulose as the raw material to prepare raw cellulose (RC) separator as the control sample. The morphologies of the OC separators are displayed in Figure S1 and S2. The presence of a small quantity of ANFs, along with the stacking effect of the coarser cellulose fibers, filled the larger pores and forms a distinctive microporous structure. This feature significantly reduces the risk of micro-short-circuiting in batteries utilizing the OC separator, enabling the utilization of cellulose fibers instead of cellulose nanofibers in separator preparation. The nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherms of the OC separator also indicate the existence of the mesoporous structure, indicating that the ANFs well-regulated the pores of the separator (Figure S4). The Fourier Transform infrared spectrometer (FTIR) and x-ray photoelectron spectra (XPS) spectrum clearly show the signals of the −COOH functional group, which proves that −COOH groups have been successfully introduced into the cellulose structure (Figure S5 and S6).<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0029_R_d104348718e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17">17</a></sup></span> All samples before and after the oxidation exhibited the diffraction peaks characteristic of cellulose, indicating that TEMPO oxidation did not alter the crystal type of cellulose (Figure S7).<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0032_R_d104348718e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Carbohydr. Polym.">18</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <p>The essential properties of the OC separator, including electrolyte compatibility, mechanical properties, and thermal stability, were evaluated to demonstrate its practical application potential. The electrolyte wetting experiment demonstrates that the contact angle of OC separator (17.1°) is significantly smaller than that of RC separator (34.4°) (Figure S8). The electrolyte uptake percentage of the OC separator is 262.9 %, much higher than of RC (209.1 %), as shown in Figure S9a. The apparently reduced contact angle and increased electrolyte uptake could be ascribed to the compatibility between the polar carboxyl groups and the ester-based electrolyte, which can result in the ideal sodiophilicity (Figure S9b).<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0033_R_d104348718e583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Nano">19</a></sup></span> The rapid electrolyte permeation, favorable wettability, and excellent compatibility with liquid electrolytes of the OC separators greatly facilitate the diffusion and kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup>, further enabling the high-rate capability of SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0034_R_d104348718e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Energy Lett.">20</a></sup></span> The mechanical properties of the separator play a crucial role in ensuring the safety of batteries during the operation process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0035_R_d104348718e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a></sup></span> Figure S9c and S10 illustrates the noteworthy high tensile strength and puncture strength exhibited by the OC separator, effectively overcoming the subpar mechanical characteristics observed in conventional GF separators. This is mainly based on the strong intermolecular hydrogen bonding among cellulose chains.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0036_R_d104348718e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Mater. Today">22</a></sup></span> In addition, the introduction of carboxyl groups also significantly increased tensile strength and puncture strength of OC separator compared to RC separator (13.65 MPa and 9.2 N mm<sup>−1</sup>), which was mainly attributed to the carboxyl groups leading to stronger intermolecular interactions between cellulose molecules, enhancing the strength of hydrogen bonds.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0037_R_d104348718e600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater.">23</a></sup></span> The OC separator exhibits less than 2 % offset at 1000 psi (approximately 6.7 MPa).<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0038_R_d104348718e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Rev.">24</a></sup></span> In order to investigate the thermal stability of the separator, heat shrinkage experiments and thermogravimetric (TG) and differential thermogram (DTG) analyzes were carried out (Figure S11). The above results confirm that OC separator has excellent electrolyte compatibility, mechanical property, and thermal stability, making it an ideal material for sodium metal batteries. Finally, a large 15*31 cm<sup>2</sup> OC separator was obtained (Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0001">1i</a>), demonstrating its mass production potential, which is expected to be widely used for Na metal batteries. This fully proves that the wet process is simple, and allows for large-scale manufacturing, which can effectively reduce costs.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202415283-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202415283-sec-0004-title"> The Influence of High Dipole Moment Separators on the Sodium Plating/Stripping Reversibility</h3>
            
            <p>To verify the enhance Na<sup>+</sup> transport rate of the above-designed high dipole moment OC separator, the experimental corroboration was carried out by Na||Na symmetric cells and Na||Cu half cells. Na|OC|Na displayed flat voltage plateaus over 1400 h with 72 mV overpotential at 0.25 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, significantly outperforming the GF separator that suffers uncontrolled Na dendrite induced short-circuit after 1017 h. In addition, the Na|RC|Na cell shows the significant voltage fluctuation after 1000 h, illustrating their poorly interfacial stability (Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0002">2a</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0039_R_d104348718e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">25</a></sup></span> The morphology of Na||Na symmetric cells was examined to further investigate the exceptional deposition behavior of the symmetric cells assembled with the OC separator. In situ morphological observation of the symmetric cells displays that Na deposition commenced as the constant current loaded, and the rapid growth of Na dendrites on the surface of metal when utilizing a RC separator (Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0002">2b</a>). In contrast, the OC separator resulted in an uniform and smooth deposition of Na over 60 min with minimal volume changes. This is consistent with our previous simulations, as the OC separator with a high dipole moment promotes uniform sodium ion flux and reduces the nucleation barrier.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0040_R_d104348718e634" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Mater.">26</a></sup></span> Depth-of-field microscopic images (Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0002">2c</a>) reveal that the sodium metal surface in symmetric batteries assembled with the RC separator appears visibly uneven, whereas those assembled with the OC separator exhibit a smooth and flat sodium surface, characterized by uniform Na deposition. SEM images further demonstrate that even after 50 cycles, the deposited Na maintains dense, flat, and large grains, whereas the sodium metal surface in the symmetric cell assembled with RC separator was rough (Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0002">2d</a>–<a href="#anie202415283-fig-0002">e</a>). This morphology is highly advantageous for reducing the area available for SEI growth and suppressing the formation of dead Na, thereby enhancing the cycling performance and battery safety.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0041_R_d104348718e646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nat. Commun.">27</a></sup></span> These findings underscore the contribution of the sodiophilic OC separator in promoting reversible Na plating/stripping behavior, which accounts for the low energy barriers and high stability in symmetric cells with the OC separator.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202415283-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/05052624-f948-4442-be78-877d035952e9/anie202415283-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/05052624-f948-4442-be78-877d035952e9/anie202415283-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/408aafe9-7d3e-420c-afd2-abad6a2e3594/anie202415283-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/05052624-f948-4442-be78-877d035952e9/anie202415283-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202415283-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202415283" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Electrochemical characterization of Na||Cu and Na||Na cells with different separators. (a) Cyclic performance of the Na|GF|Na, Na|OC|Na and Na|RC|Na symmetric cell at a current density of 0.25 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>; (b) In situ observations of the symmetric Na cells with RC or OC separator at the current density of 0.1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>; (c–e) Depth-of-field microscopic images and SEM images of the Na metal in the cycled Na|OC|Na cells and Na|RC|Na cells; (f, g) Aurbach measurement and nucleation overpotential of Na|RC|Cu and Na|OC|Cu cell at the current density of 0.5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>; (h) Voltage-capacity plots of Na||Cu cells of RC and OC separators at 0.5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>; (i) Voltage evolution of Na||Cu cells upon plating Na at 5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>; the grey dashed lines indicate the characteristic Sand's time, which is the time when the dendrite starts to grow due to complete Na<sup>+</sup> depletion.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0002">2f</a>, the Coulomb Efficiency (CE) of Na||Cu cells equipped with OC and RC separators were measured to confirm the cycling reversibility. Aurbach CE tests, which provide a more accurate evaluation of Na cycling efficiency on a Na metal substrate, further corroborate the benefit of the OC separator by revealing a substantially improved CE (98.4 %) compared to RC (91.5 %). Na||Cu half cells with the OC separator exhibited a lower nucleation barrier (Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0002">2g</a>), with a nucleation overpotential of 80.6 mV at 0.5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, a value much lower than cells containing RC separator (126.1 mV). Besides, the corresponding discharge-charge profiles of Na||Cu half cells with different separators at 0.5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> (at the 10th cycle) are presented in Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0002">2h</a>. Na||Cu half cells with OC separator displayed a smaller voltage polarization (131 mV) compared to the RC separator (251 mV). A Sand's time test was subsequently conducted at a current density of 5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> to investigate the kinetics of Na ion transport. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0002">2i</a>, after 1.14 hours of rapid deposition following Na<sup>+</sup> nucleation, the transport rate of Na<sup>+</sup> cannot follow the deposition rate and dendrites begin to grow quickly in Na|RC|Cu, which is significantly shorter that 1.85 h observed in Na|OC|Cu cells. According to the Sand's time formula, the parameters Z<sub>C</sub>, F, <i>J</i>, and C<sub>0</sub> (corresponding to the charge, Faraday constant, current density, and the concentration of Na<sup>+</sup>, respectively) are identical for two test systems, while transfer number (t<sup>+</sup>), and diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> (<i>D</i>) are the decisive factors causing the differences.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0042_R_d104348718e726" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">28</a></sup></span> Hence the ionic transport kineties of the Na||Cu cells were significantly enhanced in the presence of OC separator. These results align with the above theoretical analysis. The presence of high dipole moment OC separator, optimized the solvation structure of Na<sup>+</sup>, thereby reducing its desolvation energy barrier.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0043_R_d104348718e732" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">29</a></sup></span> Additionally, the sodiophilic nature of OC separator significantly enhanced the charge transfer kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup>, which greatly reduced the rapid growth of dendrites caused by Na depletion.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202415283-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202415283-sec-0005-title"> The Influence of the OC Separator on the SEI Composition</h3>
            
            <p>The effect of the OC separator on the chemical composition of SEI was further investigated. The high-resolution O 1s, F 1s, and Na 1s XPS spectra of the Na metal in symmetric cells after 50 cycles exhibit distinct signals, indicating that the SEI layer is primarily composed of a combination of organic components and inorganic salts (Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0003">3a</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0014_R_d104348718e749" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7a - Adv. Mater.">7a</a></sup></span> The significant differences in the inorganic salt composition of SEI using the RC and OC separator. The F 1s spectra were deconvoluted into two peaks at 683.7 and 686.7 eV, which are attributed to F−Na and F−C, respectively. The high-resolution Na 1s spectrum also shows two peaks corresponding to Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (1072 eV) and NaF/Na<sub>2</sub>O (1071.2 eV). As shown in the statistical results in Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0003">3b</a>, when using RC separator, the NaF content on the surface of the sodium anode is only 35.44 %. However, when using OC separator, the proportion of NaF in the SEI increases to 83.87 %. These results indicate that the assembly with the OC separator can induce the formation of NaF, a robust composition of SEI, with a reduced diffusion energy barrier, thereby leading to a substantial reduction in interface impedance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0044_R_d104348718e762" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30">30</a></sup></span> In addition, the SEI formed on the Na metal with the RC separator is primarily composed of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, which is formed by the reaction of NaPF<sub>6</sub> with organic solvents (EC and DEC). As the battery undergoes cycles of charging and discharging, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> on the surface of the SEI further decomposes to produce CO<sub>2</sub> gas, leading to a loose and fragile SEI layer.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0048_R_d104348718e778" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nat. Commun.">31</a></sup></span> To further elucidate the mechanism underlying of the OC separator induced generation of NaF, a simulation of the dissociation of NaPF<sub>6</sub> was carried out based on the DFT theory (Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0003">3c</a>). In the presence of the RC chain (represented as two cellulose repeating units), the dissociation energy of NaPF<sub>6</sub> producing NaF and PF<sub>5</sub> decreases from 4.21 eV to 3.95 eV. When OC separator is present, this value is further decreased to 1.33 eV. These findings elucidate that the polar hydroxyl groups present in cellulose facilitate the electron transfer, thereby enhancing the decomposition of NaPF<sub>6</sub>. The introduction of carboxyl groups with stronger dipole moments results in the transfer of more electrons to the electrolyte, which can effectively promote the breaking of P−F bonds in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and eventually form NaF. The influence of the OC separator on SEI is summarized in Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0003">3d</a> based on the analysis above. OC separator with a high dipole moment provides excess electrons to induce the generation of NaF, resulting in a dense and low-impedance SEI layer. In contrast, in the absence of OC separator, the SEI layer is mainly composed of organic matter and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, leading to an unstable SEI and higher impedance. As the unstable SEI is disrupted, Na surface is exposed and continues to participate in the reduction of the electrolyte, resulting in the continuous thickening of the SEI layer.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202415283-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/50a41f03-e541-4e71-ab6e-b90d774efdae/anie202415283-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/50a41f03-e541-4e71-ab6e-b90d774efdae/anie202415283-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2c369cc7-fb40-4c28-90dd-a98c2c726784/anie202415283-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/50a41f03-e541-4e71-ab6e-b90d774efdae/anie202415283-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202415283-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202415283" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Analysis of the SEI chemical composition and the interfacial stability. (a) High-resolution XPS spectra of the SEI in the surface of Na metal in Na|RC|Na (located in the first line) and Na|OC|Na (located in the second line) cells after 50 cycles at 0.25 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>; (b) Composition of SEI on Na metal based on XPS integral area statistics; (c) The dissociation energy of NaPF<sub>6</sub> producing NaF and PF<sub>5</sub> with the presence of RC and OC; (d) Schematic separator illustrating the formation of an unstable SEI and the promotion of NaF-rich SEI through strong dipole moment.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The organic components in SEI also profoundly affect the electrochemical behavior of cells. We disassembled Na||Cu cells that had undergone 20 charge–discharge cycles and utilized dimethyl sulfoxide-<i>d<sub>6</sub></i> solvent to extract their organic components of SEI. Subsequently, the nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) technology was employed to investigate the organic components of SEI. In the <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectrum of extracted SEI from Na|RC|Cu cells, the sodium ethyl carbonate (SEC) exhibits a quadruplet at 3.70 ppm and a triplet at 1.05 ppm (Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0004">4a</a>), and the signals of oligomers appears at 4.1, 3.5 and, 1.2 ppm.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0049_R_d104348718e843" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nano Lett.">32</a></sup></span> The formation of SEC and oligomers are illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0004">4b</a>. Near the anode interface, DEC gains electrons through an electrochemical process and is reduced to SEC. The SEC undergoes further reduction and nucleophilic attacks EC to form an intermediate, which then reacts with DEC to produce a polyethylene oxide (PEO) type oligomer. Furthermore, the excellent nucleophilicity of this intermediate leads to the lengthening reaction of PEO. A significant accumulation of SEC and oligomers forms an unstable SEI.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0050_R_d104348718e850" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">33</a></sup></span> The sodium ions are easy to combine with oligomers, which makes the sodium ion transport energy barrier larger, and a large amount of oligomers accumulate in the SEI, increasing the interfacial impedance and hindering the ion transport.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0051_R_d104348718e853" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Nano">34</a></sup></span> In contrast, the <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectrum of extracted SEI from Na|OC|Cu cells reveals almost no significant signals corresponding to SEC and oligomers. This can be attributed to the carboxyl groups introduced in the OC, which act as electron-withdrawing functional groups that attract the electrons necessary for reducing DEC, thereby terminating the formation of SEC and oligomers during the initial stages of the reaction.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202415283-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/67c45243-a0f7-4300-a4a2-7c5088479ac1/anie202415283-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/67c45243-a0f7-4300-a4a2-7c5088479ac1/anie202415283-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/45cff6dd-7f12-480d-93a1-aa1830a3ca80/anie202415283-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/67c45243-a0f7-4300-a4a2-7c5088479ac1/anie202415283-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202415283-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202415283" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Analysis of the organic composition of the SEI. (a) <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectrum of SEI formed by Na||Cu cells with RC and OC separator (using DMSO-<i>d</i><sub>6</sub>); (b) SEI formation and mechanism of oligomer formation.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202415283-sec-0006">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202415283-sec-0006-title"> The Influence of OC on the Charge Transfer Kinetics of Na||NVP Cells</h3>
            
            <p>To confirm the influence of OC separator on the transport kinetics in potential practical application, cells were assembled using Na metal, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DEC as the anode, cathode and electrolyte, respectively. Na|OC|NVP cells demonstrated a reduced charge-transfer resistance in comparison to Na|RC|NVP cell and Na|GF|NVP cell (Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0005">5a</a>), suggesting that the use of OC separator can effectively reduce the energy barrier for the charge transfer.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0052_R_d104348718e909" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Mater.">35</a></sup></span> The Na<sup>+</sup> transference numbers (t<sub>Na+</sub>) were tested through the chronoamperometry and electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS). As presented in Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0005">5b</a>, and S13, the calculated t<sub>Na+</sub> for OC is 0.82, surpassing the values for RC (0.72) and GF (0.80). The linear sweep voltammetry (LSV) test performed in Figure S14 reveals that the electrochemical voltage window of the OC separator is significantly improved, validating the enhanced electrochemical stability of Na|OC|NVP cell.<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0053_R_d104348718e922" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">36</a></sup></span> The utilization of in situ EIS testing and in situ Distribution of Relaxation Time (DRT) analysis provided a precise insight into the influence of the OC separator on the charge transfer kinetics throughout the charging and discharging processes. The Na|OC|NVP cell exhibited relatively stable impedance during cycling, indicating an excellent reversibility (Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0005">5c</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202415283-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202415283-bib-0054_R_d104348718e928" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Small">37</a></sup></span> By contrast, cells with RC and GF separators experienced substantial impedance increase, indicating an irreversible dendrite growth and SEI deterioration (Figure S15). The in situ DRT mapping of Na|OC|NVP cells throughout a cycle exhibited lower <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and R<sub>SEI</sub> values compared to Na|RC|NVP. During the charging/discharging process, the sodiophilic OC separator enhanced the Na ion flux, significantly reducing the charge transfer impedance. During the charging process, the stable NaF-rich SEI induced by OC separator reduces the transmission energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> at the interface, leading to a decrease in R<sub>SEI</sub>. The above results are consistent with the above analysis, fully demonstrating the role of reducing the energy barrier for sodium ion transport in the cells with the OC separator.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202415283-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2a717351-9c56-4379-9bc2-c094c8141bbb/anie202415283-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2a717351-9c56-4379-9bc2-c094c8141bbb/anie202415283-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b09d35b0-a63f-49b6-bd39-0421e9012b07/anie202415283-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2a717351-9c56-4379-9bc2-c094c8141bbb/anie202415283-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202415283-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202415283" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Analysis of charge transfer kinetics in Na||NVP cell. (a) Nyquist plots of Na||NVP cells with different separators; (b) Current-time (I–T) curve and the corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> transference numbers of interphases calculated from Nyquist plots; (c) In situ EIS results of the Na|OC|NVP in a voltage window between 2.5 V and 4.0 V at 0.1C; (d, e) In situ DRT data of the Na||NVP cells with d) OC separator and e) RC separator.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202415283-sec-0007">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202415283-sec-0007-title"> Cycling Performance of Na||NVP Cells with OC</h3>
            
            <p>In order to evaluate the potential application of the OC separator, we measured the cycling performance of Na||NVP cells. The cyclic voltammogram (CV) behavior of the Na||NVP cells equipped with different separators was studied within a voltage window between 2.5and 4.0 V (Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0006">6a</a>). A notable reduction in the gap between the oxidation and reduction peaks in the Na|OC|NVP cell compared to the Na|RC|NVP and Na|GF|NVP cells, indicating the efficacy of OC separator in decreasing the electrochemical polarization phenomenon in sodium metal batteries. The oxidation and reduction peaks can be attributed to the insertion and deintercalation behavior of sodium ions within the electrode active material. The Na||NVP cell with the OC separator exhibits more pronounced peaks in the CV behavior, which favorably indicates an improvement in the reversibility of redox reactions, thereby enhancing the electrochemical performance. The Na|OC|NVP cell delivers improved rate capacity at incremental scan rates and different current densities (Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0006">6b</a>–<a href="#anie202415283-fig-0006">d</a>). Specifically, the cell with the OC separator exhibits an excellent rate capacity. The discharge capacities are 114.7, 114.3, 113.2, 110.5, 108.4 and 103 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2C, 0.5C, 1C, 2C, 3C and 5C, respectively. The specific capacity recovers to 114.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> when the current density returns from 5C to 0.2C, suggesting its exceptional reversibility and stability and demonstrating its role in promoting sodium ion transfer. Moreover, the Na|OC|NVP cell exhibits a lower overpotential compared to Na|RC|NVP cells, revealing reduced internal resistance. This is mainly attributed to the OC separator with a high dipole moment, which promotes fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport and reduces the charge transfer barrier. Accordingly, the discharge voltage platforms of Na|OC|NVP cells are 3.34, 3.34, 3.33, 3.30, 3.29 and 3.26 V at 0.2C, 0.5C, 1C, 2C, 3C and 5C, all of which are higher than that of Na|RC|NVP cells (3.33, 3.32, 3.28, 3.23, 3.20 and 3.18 V at 0.2C, 0.5C, 1C, 2C, 3C and 5C, respectively). In addition, the cycling performance of the cells of different separators were evaluated at 0.5C and 1C (Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0006">6e</a>, <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0006">f</a>). Encouragingly, compared with the Na|RC|NVP and Na|GF|NVP cell, the Na||NVP cell with the OC separator demonstrates a stable cycling performance with a capacity retention of 97.5 % and a CE of 99.9 % over 500 cycles at 0.5C. Even at a higher current density of 1C, the Na|OC|NVP battery has an initial discharge specific capacity of 113.87 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and maintains a capacity of 108.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles. This observation indicates that the modification with −COOH endows the cell of the OC separator with an exceptional electrochemical stability. The residual electrolyte amount after cycling was also quantified. As depicted in Figure S16, the GF separators exhibited a shorter cycle life of 500 cycles and significant capacity reduction. Subsequently, we disassembled the batteries and measured the remained electrolyte mass. Cells equipped the OC separator have a significantly reduced consumed electrolyte content (0.058 g) compared to the initially added amount (0.1 g), and much lower than cells with GF (0.071 g) (Figure S17). Hence, the rapid growth of SEI interface and rapid consumption of electrolyte caused the decrease in local current density of the NVP cathode, leading to a decrease in capacity or even battery failure. Utilizing the OC separator can facilitate the formation of a dense and robust SEI, thereby decreasing electrolyte consumption and extending the lifetime of batteries. To verify the large-scale application potential and uniformity of the OC separator, a pouch cell was prepared. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0006">6g</a>, two layers of Al foil loaded with NVP were employed, and the OC separator was folded to wrap around the Na metal located in the middle. The discharge capacities of Na||NVP pouch cell with the OC separator were 79.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2C, with a capacity retention rate of 77 % after 400 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202415283-fig-0006">6h</a>). Moreover, the Na||NVP pouch cell steadily powered a light-emitting diode (LED) light, which demonstrated its exceptional reliability for stationary energy storage systems.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202415283-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/602c899e-cc6d-41e4-8951-ad337fdcf2ce/anie202415283-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/602c899e-cc6d-41e4-8951-ad337fdcf2ce/anie202415283-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/58bb22b3-a5f7-48fb-8e31-9bbd272ec395/anie202415283-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/602c899e-cc6d-41e4-8951-ad337fdcf2ce/anie202415283-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202415283-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202415283" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Electrochemical performance of Na||NVP cells equipped with different separators. (a) CV plots of Na|GF|NVP, Na|RC|NVP and Na|OC|NVP batteries at 0.6 mV s<sup>−1</sup>; (b, c) Voltage profiles of the Na||NVP cell at different current densities; (d) Rate performance of the Na||NVP cell of different separators at different current densities; (e) Cycling performance of Na||NVP cells with different separators at 0.5C (1C=117.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>); (f) Long-term cycling performance at a specific current of 1C; (g) Schematic structure of the prepared pouch battery; (h) Cycling performance of Na||NVP pouch cells with OC separator at 0.2C and the insert digital photo shows the LED light powered by the prepared pouch cell.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202415283-sec-0008">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202415283-sec-0008-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, we successfully designed a carboxyl functionalized cellulose separator using a wet process. The use of the OC separator facilitated homogeneous, dense, and reversible Na deposition at the anode. The −COOH group promoted the cleavage of the P−F bond, leading to the formation of a NaF-rich SEI. In addition, the separator with strong dipole moments prevented the reduction of organic solvents by attracting the required electrons in the reduction process, thereby inhibiting the formation of organic oligomers. The SEI with high NaF content and few oligomers is smooth and robust, obviously decreasing the interface impedance of the anode and extending the lifetime of Na metal batteries. The utilization of OC separator enabled the Na||Na symmetric cells to achieve highly reversible Na plating/stripping for 1400 h at 0.25 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, while the Na||NVP cell demonstrated excellent cycling stability, retaining 94.83 % of original capacity after 1000 cycles. The carboxyl functionalized cellulose separator offers a new opportunity for the design towards safe, low-cost and long lifetime Na metal batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
